<p>In the photo, the two <a href="/wiki/Batting_(cricket)" title="Batting (cricket)">batsmen</a> (3 &amp; 8; wearing yellow) have taken position at each end of the <a href="/wiki/Cricket_pitch" title="Cricket pitch">pitch</a> (6). Three members of the <a href="/wiki/Fielding_(cricket)" title="Fielding (cricket)">fielding</a> team (4, 10 &amp; 11; wearing dark blue) are in shot. One of the two umpires (1; wearing white hat) is stationed behind the <a href="/wiki/Wicket" title="Wicket">wicket</a> (2) at the <a href="/wiki/Bowling_(cricket)" title="Bowling (cricket)">bowler's</a> (4) end of the pitch. The bowler (4) is <a href="/wiki/Delivery_(cricket)" title="Delivery (cricket)">bowling</a> the <a href="/wiki/Cricket_ball" title="Cricket ball">ball</a> (5) from his end of the pitch to the batsman (8) at the other end who is called the "striker". The other batsman (3) at the bowling end is called the "non-striker". The <a href="/wiki/Wicket-keeper" title="Wicket-keeper">wicket-keeper</a> (10), who is a specialist, is positioned behind the striker's wicket (9) and behind him stands one of the fielders in a position called "<a href="/wiki/Fielding_(cricket)#Catching_positions" title="Fielding (cricket)">first slip</a>" (11). While the bowler and the first slip are wearing conventional kit only, the two batsmen and the wicket-keeper are wearing protective gear including safety helmets, padded gloves and leg guards (pads).
</p><p>While the umpire (1) in shot stands at the bowler's end of the pitch, his colleague stands in the outfield, usually in or near the fielding position called "<a href="/wiki/Square_leg" class="mw-redirect" title="Square leg">square leg</a>", so that he is in line with the <a href="/wiki/Popping_crease" class="mw-redirect" title="Popping crease">popping crease</a> (7) at the striker's end of the pitch. The bowling crease (not numbered) is the one on which the wicket is located between the return creases (12). The bowler (4) intends to hit the wicket (9) with the ball (5) or, at least, to prevent the striker (8) from scoring <a href="/wiki/Run_(cricket)" title="Run (cricket)">runs</a>. The striker (8) intends, by using his bat, to defend his wicket and, if possible, to hit the ball away from the pitch in order to score runs.
</p><p>Some players are skilled in both batting and bowling so are termed <a href="/wiki/All-rounder" title="All-rounder">all-rounders</a>. <a href="/wiki/Adam_Gilchrist" title="Adam Gilchrist">Adam Gilchrist</a>, pictured above, was a wicket-keeper/batsman, another type of all-rounder. Bowlers are also classified according to their style, generally as <a href="/wiki/Fast_bowling" class="mw-redirect" title="Fast bowling">fast bowlers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seam_bowling" title="Seam bowling">medium pace seam bowlers</a> or, like <a href="/wiki/Muttiah_Muralitharan" title="Muttiah Muralitharan">Muttiah Muralitharan</a> pictured above, <a href="/wiki/Spin_bowling" title="Spin bowling">spinners</a>. Batsmen are classified according to whether they are right-handed or left-handed.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fielding">Fielding</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Fielding_(cricket)" title="Fielding (cricket)">Fielding (cricket)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg/240px-Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg.png" decoding="async" width="240" height="321" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg/360px-Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg/480px-Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2010" data-file-height="2690" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_fielding_positions2.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fielding_positions_in_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Fielding positions in cricket">Fielding positions in cricket</a> for a right-handed batsman</div></div></div>
<p>Of the eleven fielders, three are in shot in the image above. The other eight are elsewhere on the field, their positions determined on a tactical basis by the captain or the bowler. Fielders often change position between deliveries, again as directed by the captain or bowler.<sup id="cite_ref-Law41_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law41-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>If a fielder is injured or becomes ill during a match, a <a href="/wiki/Substitute_(cricket)" title="Substitute (cricket)">substitute</a> is allowed to field instead of him, but the substitute cannot bowl or act as a captain. The substitute leaves the field when the injured player is fit to return.<sup id="cite_ref-Law2_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law2-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The Laws of Cricket were updated in 2017 to allow substitutes to act as wicket-keepers,<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup> a situation that first occurred when <a href="/wiki/Mumbai_Indians" title="Mumbai Indians">Mumbai Indians</a>' wicket-keeper Ishan Kishan was injured in a match on 18 April 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Specialist_roles">Specialist roles</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Captain_(cricket)" title="Captain (cricket)">captain (cricket)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wicket-keeper" title="Wicket-keeper">wicket-keeper</a></div>
<p>The captain is often the most experienced player in the team, certainly the most tactically astute, and can possess any of the main skillsets as a <a href="/wiki/Batsman" class="mw-redirect" title="Batsman">batsman</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Bowling_(cricket)" title="Bowling (cricket)">bowler</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Wicket-keeper" title="Wicket-keeper">wicket-keeper</a>. Within the Laws, the captain has certain responsibilities in terms of nominating his players to the umpires before the match and ensuring that his players conduct themselves "within the spirit and traditions of the game as well as within the Laws".<sup id="cite_ref-Law1_59-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law1-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The wicket-keeper (sometimes called simply the "keeper") is a specialist fielder subject to various rules within the Laws about his equipment and demeanour. He is the only member of the fielding side who can effect a <a href="/wiki/Stumped" title="Stumped">stumping</a> and is the only one permitted to wear gloves and external leg guards.<sup id="cite_ref-Law40_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law40-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Depending on their primary skills, the other ten players in the team tend to be classified as specialist batsmen or specialist bowlers. Generally, a team will include five or six specialist batsmen and four or five specialist bowlers, plus the wicket-keeper.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Clothing_and_equipment">Clothing and equipment</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cricket_clothing_and_equipment" title="Cricket clothing and equipment">Cricket clothing and equipment</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WGGrace.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/WGGrace.jpg/170px-WGGrace.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="272" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/WGGrace.jpg/255px-WGGrace.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/WGGrace.jpg/340px-WGGrace.jpg 2x" data-file-width="494" data-file-height="789" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WGGrace.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>English cricketer <a href="/wiki/W._G._Grace" title="W. G. Grace">W. G. Grace</a> "taking guard" in 1883. His pads and bat are very similar to those used today. The gloves have evolved somewhat. Many modern players use more defensive equipment than was available to Grace, notably helmets and arm guards.</div></div></div>
<p>The wicket-keeper and the batsmen wear protective gear because of the hardness of the ball, which can be delivered at speeds of more than 145 kilometres per hour (90&#160;mph) and presents a major health and safety concern. Protective clothing includes <a href="/wiki/Batting_pads" class="mw-redirect" title="Batting pads">pads</a> (designed to protect the knees and shins), <a href="/wiki/Batting_gloves" class="mw-redirect" title="Batting gloves">batting gloves</a> or <a href="/wiki/Wicket-keeper%27s_gloves" title="Wicket-keeper&#39;s gloves">wicket-keeper's gloves</a> for the hands, a <a href="/wiki/Helmet_(cricket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Helmet (cricket)">safety helmet</a> for the head and a <a href="/wiki/Box_(cricket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Box (cricket)">box</a> for male players inside the trousers (to protect the <a href="/wiki/Crotch" title="Crotch">crotch</a> area).<sup id="cite_ref-AppD_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AppD-80">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> Some batsmen wear additional padding inside their shirts and trousers such as thigh pads, arm pads, rib protectors and shoulder pads. The only fielders allowed to wear protective gear are those in positions very close to the batsman (i.e., if they are alongside or in front of him), but they cannot wear gloves or external leg guards.<sup id="cite_ref-Law41_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law41-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Subject to certain variations, on-field clothing generally includes a collared shirt with short or long sleeves; long trousers; woollen pullover (if needed); <a href="/wiki/Cricket_cap" title="Cricket cap">cricket cap</a> (for fielding) or a safety helmet; and spiked shoes or boots to increase traction. The kit is traditionally all white and this remains the case in Test and first-class cricket but, in limited overs cricket, team colours are worn instead.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Innings">Innings</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Innings" title="Innings">Innings</a></div>
<p>The innings (ending with 's' in both singular and plural form) is the term used for each phase of play during a match. Depending on the type of match being played, each team has either one or two innings. Sometimes all eleven members of the batting side take a turn to bat but, for various reasons, an innings can end before they have all done so. The innings terminates if the batting team is "all out", a term defined by the Laws: "at the <a href="/wiki/Dismissal_(cricket)" title="Dismissal (cricket)">fall of a wicket</a> or the retirement of a batsman, further balls remain to be bowled but no further batsman is available to come in".<sup id="cite_ref-inns_64-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inns-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> In this situation, one of the batsman has not been dismissed and is termed <a href="/wiki/Not_out" title="Not out">not out</a>; this is because he has no partners left and there must always be two active batsmen while the innings is in progress.
</p><p>An innings may end early while there are still two not out batsmen:<sup id="cite_ref-inns_64-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inns-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>the batting team's <a href="/wiki/Captain_(cricket)" title="Captain (cricket)">captain</a> <a href="/wiki/Declaration_and_forfeiture" title="Declaration and forfeiture">may declare the innings closed</a> even though some of his players have not had a turn to bat: this is a tactical decision by the captain, usually because he believes his team have scored sufficient runs and need time to dismiss the opposition in their innings</li>
<li>the set number of <a href="/wiki/Over_(cricket)" title="Over (cricket)">overs</a> (i.e., in a limited overs match) have been bowled</li>
<li>the match has ended prematurely due to bad weather or running out of time</li>
<li>in the final innings of the match, the batting side has reached its target and won the game.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overs">Overs</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Over_(cricket)" title="Over (cricket)">Over (cricket)</a></div>
<p>The Laws state that, throughout an innings, "the ball shall be bowled from each end alternately in overs of 6 balls".<sup id="cite_ref-Law22_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law22-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> The name "over" came about because the umpire calls "Over!" when six balls have been bowled. At this point, another bowler is deployed at the other end, and the fielding side changes ends while the batsmen do not. A bowler cannot bowl two successive overs, although a bowler can (and usually does) bowl alternate overs, from the same end, for several overs which are termed a "spell". The batsmen do not change ends at the end of the over, and so the one who was non-striker is now the striker and <i>vice-versa</i>. The umpires also change positions so that the one who was at "square leg" now stands behind the wicket at the non-striker's end and <i>vice-versa</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Law22_82-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law22-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Umpires_and_scorers">Umpires and scorers</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Umpire_(cricket)" title="Umpire (cricket)">Umpire (cricket)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scoring_(cricket)" title="Scoring (cricket)">Scoring (cricket)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cricket_statistics" title="Cricket statistics">Cricket statistics</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_Umpire.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cricket_Umpire.jpg/220px-Cricket_Umpire.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="139" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cricket_Umpire.jpg/330px-Cricket_Umpire.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Cricket_Umpire.jpg/440px-Cricket_Umpire.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1061" data-file-height="671" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_Umpire.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An umpire signals a decision to the scorers</div></div></div>
<p>The game on the field is regulated by the two <a href="/wiki/Umpire_(cricket)" title="Umpire (cricket)">umpires</a>, one of whom stands behind the wicket at the bowler's end, the other in a position called "square leg" which is about 15–20 metres away from the batsman on strike and in line with the popping crease on which he is taking guard. The umpires have several responsibilities including adjudication on whether a ball has been correctly bowled (i.e., not a <a href="/wiki/No-ball" title="No-ball">no-ball</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Wide_(cricket)" title="Wide (cricket)">wide</a>); when a run is scored; whether a batsman is out (the fielding side must first <a href="/wiki/Appeal_(cricket)" title="Appeal (cricket)">appeal</a> to the umpire, usually with the phrase "How's that?" or "Owzat?"); when intervals start and end; and the suitability of the pitch, field and weather for playing the game. The umpires are authorised to interrupt or even abandon a match due to circumstances likely to endanger the players, such as a damp pitch or deterioration of the light.<sup id="cite_ref-Law3_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law3-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Off the field in televised matches, there is usually a <a href="/wiki/Third_umpire" title="Third umpire">third umpire</a> who can make decisions on certain incidents with the aid of video evidence. The third umpire is mandatory under the playing conditions for <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test</a> and <a href="/wiki/Limited_Overs_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Limited Overs International">Limited Overs International</a> matches played between two ICC full member countries. These matches also have a <a href="/wiki/Match_referee" title="Match referee">match referee</a> whose job is to ensure that play is within the Laws and the spirit of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-Law3_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law3-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The match details, including runs and dismissals, are recorded by two official <a href="/wiki/Scoring_(cricket)" title="Scoring (cricket)">scorers</a>, one representing each team. The scorers are directed by the hand signals of an umpire (see image, right). For example, the umpire raises a forefinger to signal that the batsman is out (has been dismissed); he raises both arms above his head if the batsman has hit the ball for six runs. The scorers are required by the Laws to record all runs scored, wickets taken and overs bowled; in practice, they also note significant amounts of additional data relating to the game.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A match's <a href="/wiki/Cricket_statistics" title="Cricket statistics">statistics</a> are summarised on a scorecard. Prior to the popularisation of scorecards, most scoring was done by men sitting on vantage points cuttings notches on <a href="/wiki/Tally_stick" title="Tally stick">tally sticks</a> and runs were originally called notches.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Rowland_Bowen" title="Rowland Bowen">Rowland Bowen</a>, the earliest known scorecard templates were introduced in 1776 by T. Pratt of <a href="/wiki/Sevenoaks" title="Sevenoaks">Sevenoaks</a> and soon came into general use.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> It is believed that scorecards were printed and sold at <a href="/wiki/Lord%27s" title="Lord&#39;s">Lord's</a> for the first time in 1846.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Spirit_of_the_Game">Spirit of the Game</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_Cricket" title="Laws of Cricket">Laws of Cricket</a></div>
<p>Besides observing the Laws, cricketers must respect the "Spirit of Cricket," which is the "Preamble to the Laws," first published in the 2000 code, and updated in 2017, and now opens with this statement:<sup id="cite_ref-preamble_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-preamble-87">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<blockquote><p>"Cricket owes much of its appeal and enjoyment to the fact that it should be played not only according to the Laws, but also within the Spirit of Cricket".</p></blockquote>
<p>The Preamble is a short statement that emphasises the "Positive behaviours that make cricket an exciting game that encourages leadership,friendship and teamwork."<sup id="cite_ref-2017_changes_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2017_changes-88">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The major responsibility for ensuring fair play is placed firmly on the captains, but extends to all players, umpires, teachers, coaches and parents involved.
</p><p>The umpires are the sole judges of fair and unfair play. They are required under the Laws to intervene in case of dangerous or unfair play or in cases of unacceptable conduct by a player.
</p><p>Previous versions of the Spirit identified actions that were deemed contrary (for example, appealing knowing that the batsman is not out) but all specifics are now covered in the Laws of Cricket, the relevant governing playing regulations and disciplinary codes, or left to the judgement of the umpires, captains, their clubs and governing bodies. The terse expression of the Spirit of Cricket now avoids the diversity of cultural conventions that exist on the detail of sportsmanship – or its absence.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Bowling_and_dismissal">Bowling and dismissal</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Bowling_(cricket)" title="Bowling (cricket)">Bowling (cricket)</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dismissal_(cricket)" title="Dismissal (cricket)">Dismissal (cricket)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg/150px-Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg" decoding="async" width="150" height="213" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg/225px-Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg/300px-Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="510" data-file-height="723" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Glenn_McGrath_01_crop_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Glenn_McGrath" title="Glenn McGrath">Glenn McGrath</a> of <a href="/wiki/Australian_Cricket_Team" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian Cricket Team">Australia</a> holds the world record for most wickets in the <a href="/wiki/Cricket_World_Cup" title="Cricket World Cup">Cricket World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Most bowlers are considered specialists in that they are selected for the team because of their skill as a bowler, although some are all-rounders and even specialist batsmen bowl occasionally. The specialists bowl several times during an innings but may not bowl two overs consecutively. If the captain wants a bowler to "change ends", another bowler must temporarily fill in so that the change is not immediate.<sup id="cite_ref-Law22_82-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law22-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A bowler reaches his delivery stride by means of a "run-up" and an over is deemed to have begun when the bowler starts his run-up for the first delivery of that over, the ball then being "in play".<sup id="cite_ref-Law22_82-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law22-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Fast bowlers, needing momentum, take a lengthy run up while bowlers with a slow delivery take no more than a couple of steps before bowling. The fastest bowlers can deliver the ball at a speed of over 145 kilometres per hour (90&#160;mph) and they sometimes rely on sheer speed to try to defeat the batsman, who is forced to react very quickly.<sup id="cite_ref-pace_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pace-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> Other fast bowlers rely on a mixture of speed and guile by making the ball <a href="/wiki/Seam_bowling" title="Seam bowling">seam</a> or <a href="/wiki/Swing_bowling" title="Swing bowling">swing</a> (i.e. curve) in flight. This type of delivery can deceive a batsman into miscuing his shot, for example, so that the ball just touches the edge of the bat and can then be "caught behind" by the wicket-keeper or a slip fielder.<sup id="cite_ref-pace_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pace-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup> At the other end of the bowling scale is the <a href="/wiki/Spin_bowling" title="Spin bowling">spin bowler</a> who bowls at a relatively slow pace and relies entirely on guile to deceive the batsman. A spinner will often "buy his wicket" by "tossing one up" (in a slower, steeper <a href="/wiki/Parabolic_trajectory" title="Parabolic trajectory">parabolic</a> path) to lure the batsman into making a poor shot. The batsman has to be very wary of such deliveries as they are often "flighted" or spun so that the ball will not behave quite as he expects and he could be "trapped" into getting himself out.<sup id="cite_ref-spin_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-spin-91">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> In between the pacemen and the spinners are the medium paced seamers who rely on persistent accuracy to try to contain the rate of scoring and wear down the batsman's concentration.<sup id="cite_ref-pace_90-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pace-90">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are ten ways in which a batsman can be dismissed: five relatively common and five extremely rare. The common forms of dismissal are <a href="/wiki/Bowled" title="Bowled">bowled</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Law30_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law30-92">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caught" title="Caught">caught</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Law32_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law32-93">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leg_before_wicket" title="Leg before wicket">leg before wicket</a> (lbw),<sup id="cite_ref-Law36_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law36-94">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Run_out" title="Run out">run out</a><sup id="cite_ref-Law38_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law38-95">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Stumped" title="Stumped">stumped</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Law39_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law39-96">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup> Rare methods are <a href="/wiki/Hit_wicket" title="Hit wicket">hit wicket</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Law35_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law35-97">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hit_the_ball_twice" title="Hit the ball twice">hit the ball twice</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Law34_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law34-98">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Obstructing_the_field" title="Obstructing the field">obstructing the field</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Law37_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law37-99">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Handled_the_ball" title="Handled the ball">handled the ball</a><sup id="cite_ref-Law33_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law33-100">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Timed_out" title="Timed out">timed out</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Law31_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law31-101">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup> The Laws state that the fielding team, usually the bowler in practice, must appeal for a dismissal before the umpire can give his decision. If the batsman is out, the umpire raises a forefinger and says "Out!"; otherwise, he will shake his head and say "Not out".<sup id="cite_ref-Law27_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law27-102">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> There is, effectively, an eleventh method of dismissal, <a href="/wiki/Retired_out" class="mw-redirect" title="Retired out">retired out</a>, which is not an on-field dismissal as such but rather a retrospective one for which no fielder is credited.<sup id="cite_ref-Law2RO_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law2RO-103">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Batting.2C_runs_and_extras"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Batting,_runs_and_extras">Batting, runs and extras</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Batting_(cricket)" title="Batting (cricket)">Batting (cricket)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Run_(cricket)" title="Run (cricket)">Run (cricket)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Extra_(cricket)" title="Extra (cricket)">Extra (cricket)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_shots.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Cricket_shots.svg/170px-Cricket_shots.svg.png" decoding="async" width="170" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Cricket_shots.svg/255px-Cricket_shots.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Cricket_shots.svg/340px-Cricket_shots.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="2200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cricket_shots.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The directions in which a right-handed batsman, facing down the page, intends to send the ball when playing various cricketing shots. The diagram for a <a href="/wiki/Left-handed" class="mw-redirect" title="Left-handed">left-handed</a> batsman is a <a href="/wiki/Mirror_image" title="Mirror image">mirror image</a> of this one.</div></div></div>
<p>Batsmen take turns to bat via a <a href="/wiki/Batting_order_(cricket)" title="Batting order (cricket)">batting order</a> which is decided beforehand by the team captain and presented to the umpires, though the order remains flexible when the captain officially nominates the team.<sup id="cite_ref-Law1_59-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law1-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Substitute batsmen are not allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-Law2_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law2-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>A skilled batsman can use a wide array of "shots" or "strokes" in both defensive and attacking mode. The idea is to hit the ball to best effect with the flat surface of the bat's blade. If the ball touches the side of the bat it is called an "<a href="/wiki/Edge_(cricket)" class="mw-redirect" title="Edge (cricket)">edge</a>". The batsman does not have to play a shot and can allow the ball to go through to the <a href="/wiki/Wicketkeeper" class="mw-redirect" title="Wicketkeeper">wicketkeeper</a>. Equally, he does not have to attempt a run when he hits the ball with his bat. Batsmen do not always seek to hit the ball as hard as possible, and a good player can score runs just by making a deft stroke with a turn of the wrists or by simply "blocking" the ball but directing it away from fielders so that he has time to take a run. A wide variety of shots are played, the batsman's repertoire including strokes named according to the style of swing and the direction aimed: e.g., "<a href="/wiki/List_of_cricket_terms#Cut" class="mw-redirect" title="List of cricket terms">cut</a>", "drive", "hook", "pull".<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The batsman on strike (i.e. the "striker") must prevent the ball hitting the wicket, and try to score <a href="/wiki/Run_(cricket)" title="Run (cricket)">runs</a> by hitting the ball with his bat so that he and his partner have time to run from one end of the pitch to the other before the fielding side can return the ball. To register a run, both runners must touch the ground behind the popping crease with either their bats or their bodies (the batsmen carry their bats as they run). Each completed run increments the score of both the team and the striker.<sup id="cite_ref-Law18_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law18-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg/220px-Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg/330px-Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg/440px-Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="679" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Master_Blaster_at_work.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Sachin_Tendulkar" title="Sachin Tendulkar">Sachin Tendulkar</a> is the only player to have scored one hundred international <a href="/wiki/Century_(cricket)" title="Century (cricket)">centuries</a></div></div></div>
<p>The decision to attempt a run is ideally made by the batsman who has the better view of the ball's progress, and this is communicated by calling: usually "yes", "no" or "wait". More than one run can be scored from a single hit: hits worth one to three runs are common, but the size of the field is such that it is usually difficult to run four or more.<sup id="cite_ref-Law18_105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law18-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup> To compensate for this, hits that reach the boundary of the field are automatically awarded four runs if the ball touches the ground <i>en route</i> to the boundary or six runs if the ball clears the boundary without touching the ground within the boundary. In these cases the batsmen do not need to run.<sup id="cite_ref-Law19_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law19-106">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Hits for five are unusual and generally rely on the help of "overthrows" by a fielder returning the ball. If an odd number of runs is scored by the striker, the two batsmen have changed ends, and the one who was non-striker is now the striker. Only the striker can score individual runs, but all runs are added to the team's total.<sup id="cite_ref-Law18_105-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law18-105">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Additional runs can be gained by the batting team as <a href="/wiki/Extra_(cricket)" title="Extra (cricket)">extras</a> (called "sundries" in Australia) due to errors made by the fielding side. This is achieved in four ways: <a href="/wiki/No-ball" title="No-ball">no-ball</a>, a penalty of one extra conceded by the bowler if he breaks the rules;<sup id="cite_ref-Law24_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law24-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Wide_(cricket)" title="Wide (cricket)">wide</a>, a penalty of one extra conceded by the bowler if he bowls so that the ball is out of the batsman's reach;<sup id="cite_ref-Law25_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law25-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Bye_(cricket)" title="Bye (cricket)">bye</a>, an extra awarded if the batsman misses the ball and it goes past the wicket-keeper and gives the batsmen time to run in the conventional way;<sup id="cite_ref-Law26_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law26-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Leg_bye" title="Leg bye">leg bye</a>, as for a bye except that the ball has hit the batsman's body, though not his bat.<sup id="cite_ref-Law26_109-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law26-109">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> If the bowler has conceded a no-ball or a wide, his team incurs an additional penalty because that ball (i.e., delivery) has to be bowled again and hence the batting side has the opportunity to score more runs from this extra ball.<sup id="cite_ref-Law24_107-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law24-107">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Law25_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Law25-108">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Women.27s_cricket"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women's_cricket">Women's cricket</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_cricket" title="Women&#39;s cricket">Women's cricket</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg/220px-Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg/330px-Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg/440px-Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2112" data-file-height="1362" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Mithali_Raj_Truro_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Mithali_Raj" title="Mithali Raj">Mithali Raj</a> of India, is the only player to surpass the 6,000 run mark in <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_One_Day_International_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Women&#39;s One Day International cricket">Women's One Day International cricket</a>.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Women%27s_cricket" title="Women&#39;s cricket">Women's cricket</a> was first recorded in <a href="/wiki/Surrey" title="Surrey">Surrey</a> in 1745.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> International development began at the start of the 20th century and the first Test Match was played between <a href="/wiki/Australia_Women_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Australia Women cricket team">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/England_women%27s_cricket_team" title="England women&#39;s cricket team">England</a> in December 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-ICC20C_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICC20C-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The following year, <a href="/wiki/New_Zealand_Womens_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="New Zealand Womens cricket team">New Zealand</a> women joined them, and in 2007 <a href="/wiki/Netherlands_women_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="Netherlands women cricket team">Netherlands women</a> became the tenth women's Test nation when they made their debut against <a href="/wiki/South_Africa_women_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="South Africa women cricket team">South Africa women</a>. In 1958, the International Women's Cricket Council was founded (it merged with the ICC in 2005).<sup id="cite_ref-ICC20C_111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICC20C-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> In 1973, the first Cricket World Cup of any kind took place when a Women's World Cup was held in England.<sup id="cite_ref-ICC20C_111-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICC20C-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup>  In 2005, the <a href="/wiki/International_Women%27s_Cricket_Council" title="International Women&#39;s Cricket Council">International Women's Cricket Council</a> was merged with the International Cricket Council (ICC) to form one unified body to help manage and develop cricket. The ICC Women's Rankings were launched on 1 October 2015 covering all three formats of women's cricket. In October 2018 following the ICC's decision to award T20 International status to all members, the Women's rankings were split into separate <a href="/wiki/One_Day_International" title="One Day International">ODI</a> (for Full Members) and T20I lists.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance">Governance</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">International Cricket Council</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Cricket_Council_members_(by_status)_2017.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg/330px-International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg.png" decoding="async" width="330" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg/495px-International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg/660px-International_Cricket_Council_members_%28by_status%29_2017.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="2754" data-file-height="1398" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:International_Cricket_Council_members_(by_status)_2017.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">ICC</a> member nations. The (highest level) Test playing nations are shown in red; the associate member nations are shown in orange, with those with <a href="/wiki/One_Day_International" title="One Day International">ODI status</a> in a darker shade; suspended or former members are shown in dark grey.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Cricket_Council" title="International Cricket Council">International Cricket Council</a> (ICC), which has its headquarters in <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, is the global governing body of cricket. It was founded as the Imperial Cricket Conference in 1909 by representatives from England, Australia and South Africa, renamed the International Cricket Conference in 1965, and took up its current name in 1989.<sup id="cite_ref-ICC20C_111-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ICC20C-111">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> The ICC in 2017 has 105 member nations, twelve of which hold full membership and can play <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test cricket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> The ICC is responsible for the organisation and governance of cricket's major international tournaments, notably the men's and women's versions of the <a href="/wiki/Cricket_World_Cup" title="Cricket World Cup">Cricket World Cup</a>. It also appoints the umpires and referees that officiate at all sanctioned Test matches, Limited Overs Internationals and Twenty20 Internationals.
</p><p>Each member nation has a national cricket board which regulates cricket matches played in its country, selects the national squad, and organises home and away tours for the national team.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> In the West Indies, which for cricket purposes is a federation of nations, these matters are addressed by <a href="/wiki/Cricket_West_Indies" title="Cricket West Indies">Cricket West Indies</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The table below lists the ICC full members and their national cricket boards:<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable sortable">

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Types_of_match">Types of match</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Forms_of_cricket" title="Forms of cricket">Forms of cricket</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:England_vs_South_Africa.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/England_vs_South_Africa.jpg/220px-England_vs_South_Africa.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/England_vs_South_Africa.jpg/330px-England_vs_South_Africa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/England_vs_South_Africa.jpg/440px-England_vs_South_Africa.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:England_vs_South_Africa.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test match</a> between South Africa and England in January 2005. The men wearing black trousers are the <a href="/wiki/Umpire_(cricket)" title="Umpire (cricket)">umpires</a>. Teams in Test cricket, <a href="/wiki/First-class_cricket" title="First-class cricket">first-class cricket</a> and <a href="/wiki/Club_cricket" title="Club cricket">club cricket</a> wear traditional white uniforms and use red <a href="/wiki/Cricket_ball" title="Cricket ball">cricket balls</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Cricket is a multi-faceted sport with multiple formats that can effectively be divided into <a href="/wiki/First-class_cricket" title="First-class cricket">first-class cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Limited_overs_cricket" title="Limited overs cricket">limited overs cricket</a> and, historically, <a href="/wiki/Single_wicket_cricket" title="Single wicket cricket">single wicket cricket</a>. The highest standard is <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket" title="Test cricket">Test cricket</a> (always written with a capital "T") which is in effect the international version of first-class cricket and is restricted to teams representing the twelve countries that are full members of the ICC (see above). Although the term "Test match" was not coined until much later, Test cricket is deemed to have begun with two matches between <a href="/wiki/Australia_national_cricket_team" title="Australia national cricket team">Australia</a> and <a href="/wiki/England_national_cricket_team" class="mw-redirect" title="England national cricket team">England</a> in the <a href="/wiki/1876-77_Australian_cricket_season" class="mw-redirect" title="1876-77 Australian cricket season">1876–77 Australian season</a>; since 1882, most Test series between England and Australia have been played for a trophy known as <a href="/wiki/The_Ashes" title="The Ashes">The Ashes</a>. The term "first-class", in general usage, is applied to top-level domestic cricket. Test matches are played over five days and first-class over three to four days; in all of these matches, the teams are allotted two innings each and the draw is a valid result.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Limited overs cricket is always scheduled for completion in a single day. There are two types: <a href="/wiki/List_A_cricket" title="List A cricket">List A</a> which normally allows fifty overs per team; and <a href="/wiki/Twenty20" title="Twenty20">Twenty20</a> in which the teams have twenty overs each. Both of the limited overs forms are played internationally as <a href="/wiki/Limited_Overs_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Limited Overs International">Limited Overs Internationals</a> (LOI) and <a href="/wiki/Twenty20_International" title="Twenty20 International">Twenty20 Internationals</a> (T20I). List A was introduced in England in the 1963 season as a knockout cup contested by the first-class county clubs. In 1969, a national league competition was established. The concept was gradually introduced to the other leading cricket countries and the first limited overs international was played in 1971. In 1975, the first <a href="/wiki/Cricket_World_Cup" title="Cricket World Cup">Cricket World Cup</a> took place in England. Twenty20 is a new variant of limited overs itself with the purpose being to complete the match within about three hours, usually in an evening session. The first <a href="/wiki/Twenty20_World_Championship" class="mw-redirect" title="Twenty20 World Championship">Twenty20 World Championship</a> was held in 2007. Limited overs matches cannot be drawn, although a tie is possible and an unfinished match is a "no result".<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Single wicket was popular in the 18th and 19th centuries and its matches were generally considered top-class. In this form, although each team may have from one to six players, there is only one batsman in at a time and he must face every delivery bowled while his innings lasts. Single wicket has rarely been played since limited overs cricket began. Matches tended to have two innings per team like a full first-class one and they could end in a draw.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_competitions">International competitions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/International_cricket" title="International cricket">International cricket</a></div>
<p>Most international matches are played as parts of 'tours', when one nation travels to another for a number of weeks or months, and plays a number of matches of various sorts against the host nation. Sometimes a <a href="/wiki/Test_cricket#Perpetual_trophies" title="Test cricket">perpetual trophy</a> is awarded to the winner of the Test series, the most famous of which is <a href="/wiki/The_Ashes" title="The Ashes">The Ashes</a>.
</p><p>The ICC also organises competitions that are for several countries at once, including the <a href="/wiki/Cricket_World_Cup" title="Cricket World Cup">Cricket World Cup</a>, <a href="/wiki/ICC_T20_World_Cup" title="ICC T20 World Cup">ICC T20 World Cup</a> and <a href="/wiki/ICC_Champions_Trophy" title="ICC Champions Trophy">ICC Champions Trophy</a>. A league competition for Test matches played as part of normal tours, the <a href="/wiki/ICC_World_Test_Championship" title="ICC World Test Championship">ICC World Test Championship</a>, has been proposed several times, and is currently planned to begin in 2019. A league competition for ODIs, the <a href="/wiki/2020%E2%80%9322_ICC_Cricket_World_Cup_Super_League" title="2020–22 ICC Cricket World Cup Super League">2020–22 ICC Cricket World Cup Super League</a>, is planned to begin in 2020. The ICC maintains <a href="/wiki/ICC_Test_Championship" title="ICC Test Championship">Test rankings</a>, <a href="/wiki/ICC_ODI_Championship" title="ICC ODI Championship">ODI rankings</a> and <a href="/wiki/ICC_T20I_Championship" title="ICC T20I Championship">T20 rankings</a> systems for the countries which play these forms of cricket.
</p><p>Competitions for member nations of the ICC with <a href="/wiki/List_of_International_Cricket_Council_members#Associate_members" title="List of International Cricket Council members">Associate status</a> include the <a href="/wiki/ICC_Intercontinental_Cup" title="ICC Intercontinental Cup">ICC Intercontinental Cup</a>, for first-class cricket matches, and the <a href="/wiki/World_Cricket_League" title="World Cricket League">World Cricket League</a> for one-day matches, the final matches of which now also serve as the <a href="/wiki/ICC_World_Cup_Qualifier" title="ICC World Cup Qualifier">ICC World Cup Qualifier</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National_competitions">National competitions</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Domestic_cricket_competitions" title="Category:Domestic cricket competitions">Category:Domestic cricket competitions</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg/220px-Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg/330px-Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg/440px-Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="832" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Yorkshire-CCC-1895.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Yorkshire County Cricket Club">Yorkshire County Cricket Club</a> in 1895. The team first won the County Championship in 1893.</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="First-class">First-class</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_current_first-class_cricket_teams" title="List of current first-class cricket teams">List of current first-class cricket teams</a></div>
<p>First-class cricket in England is played for the most part by the 18 county clubs which contest the <a href="/wiki/County_Championship" title="County Championship">County Championship</a>. The concept of a <a href="/wiki/Champion_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Champion County">champion county</a> has existed since the 18th century but the official competition was not established until 1890.<sup id="cite_ref-AMCS_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AMCS-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The most successful club has been <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Yorkshire County Cricket Club">Yorkshire</a>, who had won 32 official titles (plus one shared) as of 2019.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Australia established its national first-class championship in 1892–93 when the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Shield" title="Sheffield Shield">Sheffield Shield</a> was introduced. In Australia, the first-class teams represent the various states.<sup id="cite_ref-CH175_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CH175-123">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/New_South_Wales_cricket_team" title="New South Wales cricket team">New South Wales</a> has the highest number of titles.
</p><p>The other ICC full members have national championship trophies called the <a href="/wiki/Ahmad_Shah_Abdali_4-day_Tournament" title="Ahmad Shah Abdali 4-day Tournament">Ahmad Shah Abdali 4-day Tournament</a> (Afghanistan); the <a href="/wiki/National_Cricket_League" title="National Cricket League">National Cricket League</a> (Bangladesh); the <a href="/wiki/Ranji_Trophy" title="Ranji Trophy">Ranji Trophy</a> (India); the <a href="/wiki/Inter-Provincial_Championship" title="Inter-Provincial Championship">Inter-Provincial Championship</a> (Ireland); the <a href="/wiki/Plunket_Shield" title="Plunket Shield">Plunket Shield</a> (New Zealand); the <a href="/wiki/Quaid-e-Azam_Trophy" title="Quaid-e-Azam Trophy">Quaid-e-Azam Trophy</a> (Pakistan); the <a href="/wiki/Sunfoil_Series" title="Sunfoil Series">Currie Cup</a> (South Africa); the <a href="/wiki/Premier_Trophy" title="Premier Trophy">Premier Trophy</a> (Sri Lanka); the <a href="/wiki/Regional_Four_Day_Competition" title="Regional Four Day Competition">Shell Shield</a> (West Indies); and the <a href="/wiki/Logan_Cup" title="Logan Cup">Logan Cup</a> (Zimbabwe).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Limited_overs">Limited overs</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_domestic_Twenty20_cricket_competitions" class="mw-redirect" title="List of domestic Twenty20 cricket competitions">List of domestic Twenty20 cricket competitions</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Minor_Counties_Cricket_Championship" title="Minor Counties Cricket Championship">Minor Counties Cricket Championship</a> and <a href="/wiki/Second_XI_Championship" title="Second XI Championship">Second XI Championship</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Club_and_school_cricket">Club and school cricket</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Village_cricket" title="Village cricket">Village cricket</a>, <a href="/wiki/Club_cricket" title="Club cricket">Club cricket</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Schools_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Schools cricket">Schools cricket</a></div>
<p>The world's earliest known cricket match was a <a href="/wiki/Village_cricket" title="Village cricket">village cricket</a> meeting in <a href="/wiki/Kent" title="Kent">Kent</a> which has been deduced from a 1640 court case recording a "cricketing" of "the Weald and the Upland" versus "the Chalk Hill" at <a href="/wiki/Chevening" title="Chevening">Chevening</a> "about thirty years since" (i.e., <abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1611</span>). Inter-parish contests became popular in the first half of the 17th century and continued to develop through the 18th with the first local leagues being founded in the second half of the 19th.<sup id="cite_ref-DU4_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DU4-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the grassroots level, local <a href="/wiki/Club_cricket" title="Club cricket">club cricket</a> is essentially an amateur pastime for those involved but still usually involves teams playing in competitions at weekends or in the evening. <a href="/wiki/Schools_cricket" class="mw-redirect" title="Schools cricket">Schools cricket</a>, first known in southern England in the 17th century, has a similar scenario and both are widely played in the countries where cricket is popular.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> Although there can be variations in game format, compared with professional cricket, the Laws are always observed and club/school matches are therefore formal and competitive events.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> The sport has numerous informal variants such as <a href="/wiki/French_cricket" title="French cricket">French cricket</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture">Culture</span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main page: <a href="/wiki/Category:Cricket_culture" title="Category:Cricket culture">Category:Cricket culture</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_everyday_life">Influence on everyday life</span></h3>
<p>Cricket has had a broad impact on popular culture, both in the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a> and elsewhere. It has, for example, influenced the lexicon of these nations, especially the English language, with various phrases such as "that's not cricket" (that's unfair), "had a good <a href="/wiki/Innings#Usage_outside_of_cricket" title="Innings">innings</a>" (lived a long life) and "<a href="/wiki/Sticky_wicket" title="Sticky wicket">sticky wicket</a>". "On a sticky wicket" (<i>aka</i> "sticky dog" or "glue pot")<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup> is a <a href="/wiki/Metaphor" title="Metaphor">metaphor</a><sup id="cite_ref-CalliesKeller2011_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CalliesKeller2011-128">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup> used to describe a difficult circumstance. It originated as a term for difficult batting conditions in cricket, caused by a damp and soft pitch.<sup id="cite_ref-Hendrickson2001_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hendrickson2001-129">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_arts_and_popular_culture">In the arts and popular culture</span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Cricket_in_fiction" title="Cricket in fiction">Cricket in fiction</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cricket_in_film_and_television" title="Cricket in film and television">Cricket in film and television</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cricket_poetry" title="Cricket poetry">Cricket poetry</a></div>
<p>Cricket is the subject of works by noted English poets, including <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">William Blake</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-art_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_a_Boundary" title="Beyond a Boundary">Beyond a Boundary</a></i> (1963), written by Trinidadian <a href="/wiki/C._L._R._James" title="C. L. R. James">C. L. R. James</a>, is often named the best book on any sport ever written.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg/170px-William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="261" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg/255px-William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg/340px-William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg 2x" data-file-width="395" data-file-height="607" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:William_Handcock_Tom_Wills.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Tom_Wills" title="Tom Wills">Tom Wills</a>, cricketer and co-founder of <a href="/wiki/Australian_football" class="mw-redirect" title="Australian football">Australian football</a></div></div></div>
<p>In the visual arts, notable cricket paintings include <a href="/wiki/Albert_Chevallier_Tayler" title="Albert Chevallier Tayler">Albert Chevallier Tayler</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Kent_vs_Lancashire_at_Canterbury" title="Kent vs Lancashire at Canterbury">Kent vs Lancashire at Canterbury</a></i> (1907) and <a href="/wiki/Russell_Drysdale" title="Russell Drysdale">Russell Drysdale</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cricketers" class="mw-redirect" title="The Cricketers">The Cricketers</a></i> (1948), which has been called "possibly the most famous Australian painting of the 20th century."<sup id="cite_ref-Meacham_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Meacham-132">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> French <a href="/wiki/Impressionism" title="Impressionism">impressionist</a> <a href="/wiki/Camille_Pissarro" title="Camille Pissarro">Camille Pissarro</a> painted cricket on a visit to England in the 1890s.<sup id="cite_ref-art_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Francis_Bacon_(artist)" title="Francis Bacon (artist)">Francis Bacon</a>, an avid cricket fan, captured a batsman in motion.<sup id="cite_ref-art_130-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-art-130">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a> artist <a href="/wiki/Wendy_Nanan" title="Wendy Nanan">Wendy Nanan</a>'s cricket images<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup> are featured in a limited edition first day cover for <a href="/wiki/Royal_Mail" title="Royal Mail">Royal Mail</a>'s "World of Invention" stamp issue, which celebrated the London Cricket Conference 1–3 March 2007, first international workshop of its kind and part of the celebrations leading up to the 2007 <a href="/wiki/Cricket_World_Cup" title="Cricket World Cup">Cricket World Cup</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_other_sports">Influence on other sports</span></h3>
<p>Cricket has close historical ties with <a href="/wiki/Australian_rules_football" title="Australian rules football">Australian rules football</a> and many players have <a href="/wiki/List_of_Australian_rules_footballers_and_cricketers" title="List of Australian rules footballers and cricketers">competed at top levels in both sports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> In 1858, prominent Australian cricketer <a href="/wiki/Tom_Wills" title="Tom Wills">Tom Wills</a> called for the formation of a "foot-ball club" with "a code of laws" to keep cricketers fit during the off-season. The <a href="/wiki/Melbourne_Football_Club" title="Melbourne Football Club">Melbourne Football Club</a> was founded the following year, and Wills and three other members codified the first laws of the game.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup> It is typically played on <a href="/wiki/Australian_rules_football_playing_field" title="Australian rules football playing field">modified cricket fields</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In England, a number of <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">association football</a> clubs owe their origins to cricketers who sought to play football as a means of keeping fit during the winter months. <a href="/wiki/Derby_County_F._C." class="mw-redirect" title="Derby County F. C.">Derby County</a> was founded as a branch of the <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Derbyshire County Cricket Club">Derbyshire County Cricket Club</a> in 1884;<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aston_Villa_F._C." class="mw-redirect" title="Aston Villa F. C.">Aston Villa</a> (1874) and <a href="/wiki/Everton_F._C." class="mw-redirect" title="Everton F. C.">Everton</a> (1876) were both founded by members of church cricket teams.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_United_F._C." class="mw-redirect" title="Sheffield United F. C.">Sheffield United</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Bramall_Lane" title="Bramall Lane">Bramall Lane</a> ground was, from 1854, the home of the <a href="/wiki/Sheffield_Cricket_Club" title="Sheffield Cricket Club">Sheffield Cricket Club</a>, and then of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire_County_Cricket_Club" title="Yorkshire County Cricket Club">Yorkshire</a>; it was not used for football until 1862 and was shared by Yorkshire and Sheffield United from 1889 to 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the late 19th century, a former cricketer, English-born <a href="/wiki/Henry_Chadwick_(writer)" title="Henry Chadwick (writer)">Henry Chadwick</a> of <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn" title="Brooklyn">Brooklyn</a>, New York, was credited with devising the baseball <a href="/wiki/Box_score_(baseball)" title="Box score (baseball)">box score</a><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup> (which he adapted from the cricket scorecard) for reporting game events. The first box score appeared in an 1859 issue of the <i>Clipper</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-NPR_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NPR-142">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The statistical record is so central to the game's "historical essence" that Chadwick is sometimes referred to as "the Father of Baseball" because he facilitated the popularity of the sport in its early days.<sup id="cite_ref-Myth_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myth-143">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Footnotes">Footnotes</span></h2>
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-amateur-29"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-amateur_29-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term "amateur" in this context does not mean someone who played cricket in his spare time. Many amateurs in <a href="/wiki/First-class_cricket" title="First-class cricket">first-class cricket</a> were full-time players during the cricket season. Some of the game's greatest players, including <a href="/wiki/W._G._Grace" title="W. G. Grace">W. G. Grace</a>, held amateur status.</span>
</li>
</ol></div>
